<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41588"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The maize W22 genome provides a foundation for functional genomics and transposon biology"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Genetics 2018 50:9"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2018-07-30"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2018 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="The maize W22 inbred has served as a platform for maize genetics since the mid twentieth century. To streamline maize genome analyses, we have sequenced and de novo assembled a W22 reference genome using short-read sequencing technologies. We show that significant structural heterogeneity exists in comparison to the B73 reference genome at multiple scales, from transposon composition and copy number variation to single-nucleotide polymorphisms. The generation of this reference genome enables accurate placement of thousands of Mutator (Mu) and Dissociation (Ds) transposable element insertions for reverse and forward genetics studies. Annotation of the genome has been achieved using RNA-seq analysis, differential nuclease sensitivity profiling and bisulfite sequencing to map open reading frames, open chromatin sites and DNA methylation profiles, respectively. Collectively, the resources developed here integrate W22 as a community reference genome for functional genomics and provide a foundation for the maize pan-genome. Sequencing and de novo assembly of the maize W22 reference genome enable accurate placement of Mutator (Mu) and Dissociation (Ds) transposable element insertions, providing a foundation for maize functional genomics and transposon biology."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>The maize W22 inbred has served as a platform for maize genetics since the mid twentieth century. To streamline maize genome analyses, we have sequenced and de novo assembled a W22 reference genome using short-read sequencing technologies. We show that significant structural heterogeneity exists in comparison to the B73 reference genome at multiple scales, from transposon composition and copy number variation to single-nucleotide polymorphisms. The generation of this reference genome enables accurate placement of thousands of <i>Mutator</i> (<i>Mu</i>) and <i>Dissociation</i> (<i>Ds</i>) transposable element insertions for reverse and forward genetics studies. Annotation of the genome has been achieved using RNA-seq analysis, differential nuclease sensitivity profiling and bisulfite sequencing to map open reading frames, open chromatin sites and DNA methylation profiles, respectively. Collectively, the resources developed here integrate W22 as a community reference genome for functional genomics and provide a foundation for the maize pan-genome.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The maize W22 genome has served as a foundation for maize genetics since its early adoption by Brink for studies of paramutation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Brink, R. A., Styles, E. D. &amp; Axtell, J. D. Paramutation: directed genetic change. Paramutation occurs in somatic cells and heritably alters the functional state of a locus. Science 159, 161â170 (1968)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d50179e1496">1</a></sup>. The inbred, developed at the Wisconsin Agricultural Experiment Station, lacked the ubiquitous suppressors of anthocyanin pigmentation present in most standard corn belt inbreds but did carry mutations in two regulatory genes controlling anthocyanin production, <i>R1</i> and <i>C1</i>. Thus, Brink introgressed functional alleles of <i>R1</i> and <i>C1</i> to produce a color-converted W22 inbred (PI accession <a href="https://training.arsgrin.gov/gringlobal/AccessionDetail.aspx?id=1925431">674445</a>). This inbred was soon adopted by Nelson, Kermicle and Dooner and their students, who led foundational studies to understand the genetics of high-protein maize (<i>O2</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Mertz, E. T., Bates, L. S. &amp; Nelson, O. E. Mutant gene that changes protein composition and increases lysine content of maize endosperm. Science 145, 279â280 (1964)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d50179e1523">2</a></sup>, provide the first example of imprinting<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Kermicle, J. L. Dependence of the R-mottled aleurone phenotype in maize on mode of sexual transmission. Genetics 66, 69â85 (1970)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d50179e1527">3</a></sup>, conduct the first transposon tagging experiments in plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Fedoroff, N. V., Furtek, D. B. &amp; Nelson, O. E. Cloning of the bronze locus in maize by a simple and generalizable procedure using the transposable controlling element Activator (Ac). Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 81, 3825â3829 (1984)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d50179e1531">4</a></sup> and understand the mechanisms of <i>Activator</i> and <i>Dissociation</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Chen, J., Greenblatt, I. M. &amp; Dellaporta, S. L. Transposition of Ac from the P locus of maize into unreplicated chromosomal sites. Genetics 117, 109â116 (1987)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d50179e1541">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Dooner, H., English, J., Ralston, E. &amp; Weck, E. A single genetic unit specifies two transposition functions in the maize element activator. Science 234, 210â211 (1986)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d50179e1544">6</a></sup> transposition. Today, the W22 genome is the host to thousands of mobilized, indexed <i>Mutator</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="McCarty, D. R. et al. Steady-state transposon mutagenesis in inbred maize. Plant J. 44, 52â61 (2005)." href="#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d50179e1550">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="McCarty, D. R. et al. Mu-seq: sequence-based mapping and identification of transposon induced mutations. PLoS One 8, e77172 (2013)." href="#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d50179e1550_1">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Liu, P., McCarty, D. R. &amp; Koch, K. E. Transposon mutagenesis and analysis of mutants in UniformMu maize (Zea mays). Curr. Protoc. Plant Biol. 1, 451â465." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d50179e1553">9</a></sup>, <i>Activator</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Brutnell, T. P. &amp; Conrad, L. J. Transposon tagging using Activator (Ac) in maize. Methods Mol. Biol. 236, 157â176 (2003)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d50179e1559">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Kolkman, J. M. et al. Distribution of Activator (Ac) throughout the maize genome for use in regional mutagenesis. Genetics 169, 981â995 (2005)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d50179e1562">11</a></sup> and <i>Dissociation</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Li, Y., Segal, G., Wang, Q. &amp; Dooner, H. K. Gene tagging with engineered Ds elements in maize. Methods Mol. Biol. 1057, 83â99 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d50179e1568">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Vollbrecht, E. et al. Genome-wide distribution of transposed Dissociation elements in maize. Plant Cell 22, 1667â1685 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d50179e1571">13</a></sup> insertions that are used extensively for functional genomics studies. Maize exhibits exceptional levels of structural variation that influences gene content and gene regulation among different inbred lines<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Springer, N. M. et al. Maize inbreds exhibit high levels of copy number variation (CNV) and presence/absence variation (PAV) in genome content. PLoS Genet. 5, e1000734 (2009)." href="#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d50179e1576">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lai, J. et al. Genome-wide patterns of genetic variation among elite maize inbred lines. Nat. Genet. 42, 1027â1030 (2010)." href="#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d50179e1576_1">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Fu, H. &amp; Dooner, H. K. Intraspecific violation of genetic colinearity and its implications in maize. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 99, 9573â9578 (2002)." href="#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d50179e1576_2">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Hirsch, C. N. et al. Insights into the maize pan-genome and pan-transcriptome. Plant Cell 26, 121â135 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d50179e1579">17</a></sup>. This limits the utility of the B73 reference genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Improved maize reference genome with single-molecule technologies. Nature 546, 524â527 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d50179e1583">18</a></sup> or the PH207 genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Hirsch, C. N. et al. Draft assembly of elite inbred line PH207 provides insights into genomic and transcriptome diversity in maize. Plant Cell 28, 2700â2714 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d50179e1587">19</a></sup> for interpreting data derived for mutant alleles in a W22 genetic background. Detailed knowledge of the W22 genome structure and content is critical to fully exploit reverse genetics resources and biological processes in W22.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="W22 genome de novo assembly and validation"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">W22 genome de novo assembly and validation</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><p>The W22 genome was sequenced and assembled using deep sequencing (â&gt;â180Ã) of Illumina short-read sequences from libraries with a variety of insert sizes using the assembly algorithms developed by NRGene<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Hirsch, C. N. et al. Draft assembly of elite inbred line PH207 provides insights into genomic and transcriptome diversity in maize. Plant Cell 28, 2700â2714 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d50179e1599">19</a></sup> (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#Sec6">Methods</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#MOESM2">Reporting Summary</a> for details). Sequence contigs were developed based on paired-end (PE) short reads and then were put into scaffolds using PE or mate-pair (MP) links. Further ordering and orientation of scaffolds was achieved through alignment to the B73 genome assembly. The initial assembly was improved through utilization of the 10Ãâgenomics library platform<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Zheng, G. X. Y. et al. Haplotyping germline and cancer genomes with high-throughput linked-read sequencing. Nat. Biotechnol. 34, 303â311 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d50179e1609">20</a></sup> and sequencing using the HiSeq X10 to bridge contigs and correct initial misassemblies (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#Sec6">Methods</a>). This revealed 34 potential chimeric scaffolds that were broken and reassembled to generate a W22v2 genome (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#Tab1">1</a>; see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#Sec6">Methods</a>). The W22 whole genome is available at NCBI under the accession <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/assembly/GCA_001644905.2/">GCA_001644905.2</a>. The genome and accompanying resources are available at MaizeGDB under the name Zm-W22-REFERENCE-NRGENE-2.0 with the identifier Zm00004b.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Summary statistics for the W22v2 assembly</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Several approaches were used to assess the quality and completeness of the W22 genome sequence at macro and micro scales. To confirm the overall quality of the sequence assembly, we created an independent optical map based on the restriction site BspQI<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Luo, M.-C. et al. Optical nano-mapping and analysis of plant genomes. Methods Mol. Biol. 1429, 103â117 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d50179e1769">21</a></sup> and aligned the optical map with an in silico digested version of the genome assembly. The two assemblies strongly supported each other, showing exceptional (&gt;98%) alignment and colinearity. Further confirmation of the assembly was performed using a suite of 4.4 million SNP markers validated against the maize nested association mapping panel<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Lu, F. et al. High-resolution genetic mapping of maize pan-genome sequence anchors. Nat. Commun. 6, 6914 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d50179e1773">22</a></sup>. A genetic assessment of contig and scaffold ordering was conducted by aligning 4.4 million SNP markers to an initial W22 assembly scaffold revealing ~30 inconsistencies (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#Sec6">Methods</a>). This genetic map was used to correct the initial assembly to produce a W22v2 assembly that integrates the genetic and physical maps and provided evidence for robust assembly. The W22v2 genome has a larger number of gaps (as defined by &gt;10Ns) than the B73v4 genome but substantially fewer gaps than the PH207 genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#MOESM1">1</a>).</p><p>A more granular assessment of the assembly quality and accuracy was made by comparing the W22v2 assembly to a 238-kilobase (kb) interval encompassing the W22-<i>bz1</i> locus on chromosome 9 that had previously been sequenced using Sanger sequencing technology<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Dooner, H. K. &amp; He, L. Maize genome structure variation: interplay between retrotransposon polymorphisms and genic recombination. Plant Cell 20, 249â258 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d50179e1789">23</a></sup>. The vast majority (~113âMb) of this region aligned withâ&gt;â99% identity in a collinear fashion (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#Fig1">1</a>). The primary difference between the W22v2 genome assembly and the earlier Sanger BAC assembly is an unfilled gap present in the W22v2 assembly that corresponds to 7,137âbase pairs (bp) of a <i>Misfit</i> retrotransposon that is located in the midst of an 85-kb retrotransposon cluster (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#Fig1">1</a>). There are three other small gaps in this region that occur within repetitive elements but the size of these gaps is similar to the BAC sequence and the order and orientation is the same as the BAC. This analysis provides further evidence for the quality of the W22v2 assembly and suggests that remaining gaps likely occur within repetitive regions.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-018-0158-0/MediaObjects/41588_2018_158_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-018-0158-0/MediaObjects/41588_2018_158_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="246" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Benchmarking the W22v2 assembly quality and completeness. A comparison of the W22v2 assembly with a Sanger-based assembly of a 238-kb maize BAC<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Dooner, H. K. &amp; He, L. Maize genome structure variation: interplay between retrotransposon polymorphisms and genic recombination. Plant Cell 20, 249â258 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d50179e1813">23</a></sup> (Genbank; <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/EU338354">EU338354</a>). This region contains five genes (yellow/tan symbols along line) as well as a number of transposons (colored triangles). The W22v2 assembly matches the majority of the sequence at quite high identity (&gt;99%) and the locations of gaps in the W22v2 genome (indicated by red dotted lines).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec3" data-title="Annotation of genes and transposons in W22"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec3-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec3">Annotation of genes and transposons in W22</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec3-content"><p>Genes of the W22v2 genome were annotated using a modified Maker P approach that was developed for B73v4<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Improved maize reference genome with single-molecule technologies. Nature 546, 524â527 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d50179e1840">18</a></sup> together with RNA-seq from W22 tissues and full-length cDNA data (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#Sec6">Methods</a> for details). A âworking gene setâ of 57,181 genes was identified. Removal of putative transposons, low-confidence single-exon genes and potential pseudogenes resulted in a âfiltered gene setâ of 40,789 genes. The exons of the filtered gene set account for 2.99% and the introns account for another 5.15% of the 2.2âGb W22v2 assembly. The number of genes and distribution along the chromosomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#Fig2">2</a>) are very similar to previous maize genome assemblies for B73<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Fedoroff, N. V., Furtek, D. B. &amp; Nelson, O. E. Cloning of the bronze locus in maize by a simple and generalizable procedure using the transposable controlling element Activator (Ac). Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 81, 3825â3829 (1984)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d50179e1850">4</a></sup> and PH207<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Hirsch, C. N. et al. Draft assembly of elite inbred line PH207 provides insights into genomic and transcriptome diversity in maize. Plant Cell 28, 2700â2714 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d50179e1854">19</a></sup>. The quality and completeness of the W22 genome was assessed through a BUSCO analysis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="SimÃ£o, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d50179e1859">24</a></sup>. Over 90% of BUSCO genes are represented by a complete single copy in the W22 genome and only 3% of BUSCO genes are missing in the W22v2 assembly (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#Fig6">1</a>). These rates are equivalent to the B73v4 genome (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#Fig6">1</a>), suggesting similar qualities of annotation completeness and quality in these genomes.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-018-0158-0/MediaObjects/41588_2018_158_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-018-0158-0/MediaObjects/41588_2018_158_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="118" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Distribution of features within the W22 genome. The relative density for a variety of features in 1âMb bins across the maize genome is shown. The purple tracks show the density of genes, LTR transposons and TIR transposons. The relative chromatin accessibility as assessed by MNase (green) and the levels of CG, CHG and CHH methylation (red) are shown. The frequencies of <i>Ds</i> or <i>Mu</i> transposon insertions are also shown (gray).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The availability of a de novo assembly for the W22 genome allows for comparisons of gene copy number, structure and splicing between W22 and B73. Previous work has found evidence for frequent copy number variation and presence/absence variation among maize inbreds<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Hirsch, C. N. et al. Insights into the maize pan-genome and pan-transcriptome. Plant Cell 26, 121â135 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d50179e1896">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Hirsch, C. N. et al. Draft assembly of elite inbred line PH207 provides insights into genomic and transcriptome diversity in maize. Plant Cell 28, 2700â2714 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d50179e1899">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Swanson-Wagner, R. A. et al. Pervasive gene content variation and copy number variation in maize and its undomesticated progenitor. Genome Res. 20, 1689â1699 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d50179e1902">25</a></sup>. The B73 and W22 genomes were compared to each other and to the <i>Sorghum</i> BTx623 genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="McCormick, R. F. et al. The Sorghum bicolor reference genome: improved assembly, gene annotations, a transcriptome atlas, and signatures of genome organization. Plant J. 93, 338â354 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d50179e1909">26</a></sup> to document the presence or absence of syntenic orthologs among these three genomes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#MOESM1">2</a>). Over 60% of the genes are present at syntenic locations in both maize lines and <i>Sorghum</i>. Another 15â20% of genes are found in both B73 and W22 but are absent from the BTx623 assembly (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#MOESM1">2</a>). The remaining genes are present in one maize line and absent in the other. The majority of these genes are present only in B73 (6,440) or W22 (8,372) and not found in the other maize inbred or in <i>Sorghum</i>. There are relatively fewer examples of genes that are present in <i>Sorghum</i> and only one maize inbred (B73, 881; W22, 1,412). The presence of locally duplicated genes was evaluated in both the W22 and B73 genome, for a range of intervening genes from zero (tandem duplicates) to twenty (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#MOESM1">3 and </a><a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#MOESM1">4</a>). A subset (678) of the examples of tandem duplications are observed in both B73 and W22. However, there are many other cases of tandem duplication that are observed only in B73 (1,394) or in W22 (1,261). The tandem duplicates were classified according to their relative arrangement (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#MOESM1">5</a>). Tandem duplicates in the same orientation (head to tail) are more common than genes in a convergent (tail-to-tail) or divergent (head-to-head) arrangement (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#MOESM1">5</a>).</p><p>The presence of a tandem duplication in one genome and not in the other could reflect mechanisms to rapidly expand or contract gene copy variants and may serve as a foundation for functional diversification; for example, the terpene synthase family has variable tandem duplications that may affect function (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a> for details).</p><p>RNA-seq data generated from several W22 tissues were used to document examples of alternative splicing in W22 and compare these alternative splicing events to those observed in B73<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Mei, W. et al. A comprehensive analysis of alternative splicing in paleopolyploid maize. Front. Plant Sci. 8, 694 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d50179e1950">27</a></sup>. We identified 13,591 W22 genes with alternative splicing, representing 49.4% of expressed multi-exon genes. The types, and frequencies, of alternative splicing events noted in W22 (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#MOESM1">6</a>) are similar to those noted for B73<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Mei, W. et al. A comprehensive analysis of alternative splicing in paleopolyploid maize. Front. Plant Sci. 8, 694 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d50179e1957">27</a></sup>, albeit at lower numbers, which may be due to reduced levels of RNA-seq data for W22 relative to B73. There are numerous locus-specific differences in splicing observed between B73 and W22 and these are found distributed throughout the genome (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#Fig7">2</a>). One major advantage of sequencing and annotating additional genotypes is the improved ability to analyze high-throughput sequencing data sets. To test the benefit of using a genotype-matched reference, W22 leaf RNA sequencing data (SRA: <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/SRR1986376">SRR1986376</a>) was analyzed in parallel using both the W22 and the B73v4 genome references. Mapping RNA-seq reads from W22 seedling leaf tissue to the W22 genome resulted in a mapping rate of 95.7% while only 91.1% of these reads could be aligned to the B73v4 genome (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#Fig8">3a</a>). A comparison of the expression level for orthologous genes in B73 and W22 reveals that the expression level estimates vary for a number of genes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#Fig8">3b</a>). In many cases, these changes in expression were due to altered annotation of the transcripts in W22 relative to B73 (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#Fig8">3c</a>).</p><p>The maize genome is largely composed of transposable elements (TEs)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Schnable, P. S. et al. The B73 maize genome: complexity, diversity, and dynamics. Science 326, 1112â1115 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d50179e1985">28</a></sup>. A structural annotation of transposons in the W22v2 genome identified over 177,000 transposons that were classified into 26,833 families (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#Sec6">Methods</a>). Long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons are the most prevalent type of TEs in the W22 genome with 23,144 families accounting for 64% of the genome. The 10 most abundant LTR families have 1,400â16,395 members and account for 36% of the genome. Terminal inverted repeat (TIR) DNA transposons were classified into 5 major types and account for 0.46% of the genome. There are also 23,895 helitron elements that account for 4.6% of the genome. While many of the TEs are present in intergenic space, there are numerous examples of transposons being annotated within the introns of genes. Nearly 9% of the FGS genes contain an annotated transposon, including 1,626 genes containing a TIR element and 1,864 genes containing an LTR element. A comparison of the TEs present in the B73 and W22 genome revealed high levels of variation in TE presence and copy number for both TIR and LTR families. There are 937 families of TIR elements that are found in both B73 and W22. Many other TIR families are found only in B73 (107 families) or W22 (62 families) after excluding B73 families that are present only as MITEs. These B73-specific and W22-specific TIR elements are enriched for CACTA (DTC) families (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#Fig9">4a</a>). There are 12,740 families of LTR elements annotated in both B73 and W22 and many other families specific to W22 (10,531) or B73 (11,032). The number of elements in each family is generally similar in the two genomes but there are many examples of strong copy number variation (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#Fig9">4bâd</a>). However, there are 23 LTR families and 30 TIR families with differences in copy number &gt;100 between the two genomes including 27 with higher copy number in W22 and 26 with higher copy number in B73. The analysis of age of LTR elements (based on sequence identity of the two LTRs) in families that are inbred-specific reveals that small inbred-specific families tend to be relatively old and thus may be being purged from the genome while larger inbred specific families tend to be enriched for recent insertion events (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#Fig9">4e</a>). Even in TIR and LTR families with similar copy number we see variation for specific elements. For example, among the 100 TIR families with a single copy in each genome there are 74 examples in which the transposon is inserted into different chromosomes in the two inbred lines. A detailed analysis of native <i>Mutator</i> insertions in the B73 and W22 genomes (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a> for details) revealed similar numbers in both genomes (258 in W22 and 259 in B73). However, fewer than half (125) of these insertions are shared in both lines. There are over 100 unique <i>Mutator</i> insertions in each line and 63% of these are located within 500âbp of an annotated gene. Among the 7,463 LTR families with a single copy in both B73 and W22 there are 863 examples in which that single element is located on different chromosomes in the two genomes. These examples of polymorphic TIR and LTR elements highlight the exceptional diversity of TE insertion events among maize genotypes.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec4" data-title="Mapping of transposon insertion sites using the W22 genome"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec4-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec4">Mapping of transposon insertion sites using the W22 genome</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec4-content"><p>Significant efforts have been dedicated to the development of stocks with novel transposon insertions in the W22 genetic background. These include several approaches that have mobilized <i>Ds</i> elements<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Vollbrecht, E. et al. Genome-wide distribution of transposed Dissociation elements in maize. Plant Cell 22, 1667â1685 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2022">13</a></sup> and the development a large number of <i>Mu</i> insertion stocks<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="McCarty, D. R. et al. Steady-state transposon mutagenesis in inbred maize. Plant J. 44, 52â61 (2005)." href="#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2029">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="McCarty, D. R. et al. Mu-seq: sequence-based mapping and identification of transposon induced mutations. PLoS One 8, e77172 (2013)." href="#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2029_1">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Liu, P., McCarty, D. R. &amp; Koch, K. E. Transposon mutagenesis and analysis of mutants in UniformMu maize (Zea mays). Curr. Protoc. Plant Biol. 1, 451â465." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2032">9</a></sup>. A large number of these novel insertions have been sequence indexed to document information on the sequence flanking insertion sites. To date, the insertion sites have often been determined through mapping of flanking sequences to the B73 genome. However, a large number of insertions could not be accurately placed due to insufficient sequence identity with B73. To precisely position the 2,029 <i>Ds</i> and 68,866 <i>Mu</i> insertions that have been generated, sequences adjacent to the transposon were mapped against the W22v2 genome. This provided novel insertion sites for 12%â15% of these insertions (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#Fig3">3</a>). For the 13,444 UniformMu stocks, the mapping of flanking sites to the W22 genome provided novel insertion site information for over 10,000 insertions and increased the properly placed insertions from 4.35 per line to 5.1 per line. Furthermore, the genomic distributions of <i>Ds</i> and <i>Mu</i> insertions are distinct from one another (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#Fig2">2</a>), indicating complementarity of these resources for functional genomics studies. The improved mapping resolution of these insertions coupled with a better resolution of gene content and position afforded by W22v2 assembly will greatly increase the utility of the <i>Ds</i> and <i>Mu</i> reverse genetics resources. In particular, the <i>Ds</i> resource is designed for remobilization of elements to flanking regions of the genome, so accurate placement of insertions to gene models and local gene content is imperative for efficient mutagenesis. Similarly, better gene models will enable a more accurate prediction of phenotypic consequences of transposon insertions, particularly those that map to the 5Ê¹ and 3Ê¹ ends of a gene and thus may induce subtle mutant phenotypes.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-018-0158-0/MediaObjects/41588_2018_158_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-018-0158-0/MediaObjects/41588_2018_158_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="322" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Improved ability to document the location of <i>Ds</i> and <i>Mu</i> insertions using the W22 genome. The <i>Ds</i> (<i>n</i>â=â2,029) and <i>Mu</i> (<i>n</i>â=â68,866) insertion flanking sequences were mapped to the W22 genome. The proportion of insertion sites that mapped to the same location (black), different locations (blue) or only to W22 (red) was determined.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec5" data-title="&#xA;                        Ds and Mu probe different compartments of the genome"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec5-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec5">
                        <i>Ds</i> and <i>Mu</i> probe different compartments of the genome</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec5-content"><p><i>Ds</i> and <i>Mu</i> represent two different transposon superfamilies (DTA and DTM). Previous studies have suggested differences in terms of the frequency of linked transposition and insertion locations relative to genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Vollbrecht, E. et al. Genome-wide distribution of transposed Dissociation elements in maize. Plant Cell 22, 1667â1685 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2122">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Dietrich, C. R. et al. Maize Mu transposons are targeted to the 5Ê¹ untranslated region of the gl8 gene and sequences flanking Mu target-site duplications exhibit nonrandom nucleotide composition throughout the genome. Genetics 160, 697â716 (2002)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2125">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Liu, S. et al. Mu transposon insertion sites and meiotic recombination events co-localize with epigenetic marks for open chromatin across the maize genome. PLoS Genet. 5, e1000733 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2128">30</a></sup>. Several properties of chromatin in the W22 genome were analyzed to better understand the factors that influence the insertion sites for <i>Ds</i> and <i>Mu</i>. The context-specific DNA methylation patterns throughout the W22 genome were documented using whole-genome bisulfite sequencing (WGBS) and chromatin accessibility was assessed using MNase treatments (Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#Fig2">2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#Fig4">4aâc</a>). The levels and genomic distribution of DNA methylation in W22 are similar to patterns observed in other maize inbred lines<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Gent, J. I. et al. CHH islands: de novo DNA methylation in near-gene chromatin regulation in maize. Genome Res. 23, 628â637 (2013)." href="#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2145">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Regulski, M. et al. The maize methylome influences mRNA splice sites and reveals widespread paramutation-like switches guided by small RNA. Genome Res. 23, 1651â1662 (2013)." href="#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2145_1">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Li, Q. et al. Examining the causes and consequences of context-specific differential DNA methylation in maize. Plant Physiol. 168, 1262â1274 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2148">33</a></sup>. Open chromatin and CHH methylation are substantially enriched in promoter regions and following the transcription termination sites of annotated genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#Fig4">4a</a>). In contrast, CHG and CG methylation are relatively depleted in these regions (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#Fig4">4a</a>). CG and CHG methylation are enriched within TIR and LTR transposons (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#Fig4">4b,c</a>). CHH methylation is enriched at TIR elements but shows minimal enrichment at LTR elements. Chromatin accessibility does not vary substantially within or near transposons in the W22 genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#Fig4">4b,c</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Fig. 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-018-0158-0/MediaObjects/41588_2018_158_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-018-0158-0/MediaObjects/41588_2018_158_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="439" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Patterns of chromatin over maize genes and transposons. <b>a</b>â<b>c</b>, The levels of CG methylation (black), CHG methylation (blue) or CHH methylation (red) over the 1âkb flanking regions and maize genes (<b>a</b>), TIR transposons (<b>b</b>) or LTR transposons (<b>c</b>). In addition, the enrichment for open chromatin (green) based on MNase digests is shown for the same regions. <b>d</b>â<b>f</b>, Chromatin profiles for sites targeted by <i>Ds</i> (solid lines) or <i>Mu</i> (dashed lines). The relative levels of CG methylation (<b>d</b>) and CHG methylation (<b>e</b>) or open chromatin (<b>f</b>) are shown for the flanking regions (up to 10âkb) near Ds or Mu insertion sites.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The locations for novel transposon insertions can be quite sensitive to chromatin structure<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Sultana, T., Zamborlini, A., Cristofari, G. &amp; Lesage, P. Integration site selection by retroviruses and transposable elements in eukaryotes. Nat. Rev. Genet. 18, 292â308 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2224">34</a></sup>. The profiles of open chromatin and DNA methylation were assessed for the regions flanking potential <i>Ds</i> and <i>Mu</i> insertion sites (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#Fig4">4dâf</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#Fig10">5</a>). There are differences in the accessibility of chromatin at <i>Mu</i> and <i>Ds</i> insertion sites. The <i>Mu</i> insertion sites are marked by an increase in chromatin accessibility while <i>Ds</i> insertion sites do not vary substantially compared to flanking regions. Both <i>Mu</i> and <i>Ds</i> prefer to insert in regions with very low CG and CHG methylation. A metaprofile from bisulfite sequencing reveals that <i>Mu</i> insertion sites are often very near regions with CG or CHG methylation as both of these modifications are present on either side of the insertion site. In contrast, the increase in CG or CHG methylation near <i>Ds</i> insertion sites tends to occur further away from the insertion site. CHH methylation tends to be enriched at regions immediately adjacent to <i>Mu</i> insertion sites but does not show strong patterns near <i>Ds</i> insertion sites (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#Fig10">5</a>). These patterns could explain the preference for Mu to insert in the 5Ê¹ and 3Ê¹âUTR regions of genes while <i>Ds</i> is more likely to insert within coding regions.</p><p>The W22 genome contains a number of native TIR transposon families. The patterns of chromatin within these TIR elements include elevated DNA methylation and relatively closed chromatin (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#Fig4">4b</a>). The chromatin at the regions flanking these TIR elements likely reflects endogenous genomic information that influences chromatin state as well as the influences of the TE on neighboring chromatin. Given the differences in chromatin profiles for the insertion sites of <i>Ds</i> or <i>Mu</i>, we were interested in comparing the chromatin at these regions with the chromatin flanking pre-existing TIR insertions. We collected chromatin profiles for 2âkb of flanking regions for all TIR families with &gt;50 elements and clustered these profiles together with the profile of chromatin flanking potential <i>Ds</i> and <i>Mu</i> insertion sites (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#Fig5">5</a>). There are several quite distinct patterns of chromatin flanking TIR elements. Some families show very high methylation for flanking regions while other families are more likely to have low levels of CG and CHG methylation. The <i>Mu</i> and <i>Ds</i> potential insertion sites are outliers for the levels of CG and CHG methylation compared to the regions flanking pre-existing element insertion sites. The analysis of the methylation levels for the first 100âbp at the edge of the TE reveals that both <i>Mu</i> and <i>Ds</i> insertion sites have much lower levels of CG and CHG methylation than observed for pre-existing TIR insertion sites (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#Fig5">5b,c</a>). In contrast, the levels of CHH methylation near <i>Mu</i> and <i>Ds</i> potential insertion sites are very similar to the levels of CHH methylation at pre-existing TIR insertion sites (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#Fig5">5d</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Fig. 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-018-0158-0/MediaObjects/41588_2018_158_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-018-0158-0/MediaObjects/41588_2018_158_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="579" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Chromatin profiles for regions flanking pre-existing TIR elements or novel <i>Ds</i> or <i>Mu</i> insertion sites. <b>a</b>, For 191 TIR families with at least 20 elements present in the W22 genome, we determined the relative level of CG and CHG methylation in 100-bp windows for the 1-kb flanking regions. These levels of methylation were used to cluster the families and a heatmap allows visualization of the patterns of DNA methylation for flanking regions. <b>b</b>,<b>c</b>, The distributions of the average level of methylation for CG (<b>b</b>) or CHG (<b>c</b>) and CHH (<b>d</b>) contexts in the 100âbp surrounding each TE family are shown as density plots. The levels of methylation at sites where <i>Mu</i> (red arrows) or <i>Ds</i> (black arrows) elements can insert are shown as arrows.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Since the turn of the twentieth century, maize has provided a model system for genetic research<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Nannas, N. J. &amp; Dawe, R. K. Genetic and genomic toolbox of Zea mays. Genetics 199, 655â669 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2377">35</a></sup>. The development of a color-converted W22 inbred enabled high-resolution mapping of numerous traits influencing anthocyanin and flavonoid accumulation and led to the adoption of W22 as the workhorse of maize genetics<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Brink, R. A., Styles, E. D. &amp; Axtell, J. D. Paramutation: directed genetic change. Paramutation occurs in somatic cells and heritably alters the functional state of a locus. Science 159, 161â170 (1968)." href="#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2381">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Mertz, E. T., Bates, L. S. &amp; Nelson, O. E. Mutant gene that changes protein composition and increases lysine content of maize endosperm. Science 145, 279â280 (1964)." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2381_1">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kermicle, J. L. Dependence of the R-mottled aleurone phenotype in maize on mode of sexual transmission. Genetics 66, 69â85 (1970)." href="#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2381_2">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Fedoroff, N. V., Furtek, D. B. &amp; Nelson, O. E. Cloning of the bronze locus in maize by a simple and generalizable procedure using the transposable controlling element Activator (Ac). Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 81, 3825â3829 (1984)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2384">4</a></sup>. In recent years the genomics data sets generated from studies of transposon insertion alleles have been analyzed in the context of the B73 reference genome. Numerous studies have documented the widespread genetic variation among inbred genotypes of maize including SNPs, copy number variation and indels<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Lai, J. et al. Genome-wide patterns of genetic variation among elite maize inbred lines. Nat. Genet. 42, 1027â1030 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2388">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Hirsch, C. N. et al. Draft assembly of elite inbred line PH207 provides insights into genomic and transcriptome diversity in maize. Plant Cell 28, 2700â2714 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2391">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Messing, J. &amp; Dooner, H. Organization and variability of the maize genome. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 9, 157â163 (2006)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2394">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Chia, J. M. et al. Maize HapMap2 identifies extant variation from a genome in flux. Nat. Genet. 44, 803â807 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2397">37</a></sup>. The availability of a high-quality assembly and annotation for W22 will increase the resolution of studies of mutant alleles in this genetic background and recent improvements in maize transformation are likely to enable higher efficiencies of W22 transformation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Altpeter, F. et al. Advancing crop transformation in the era of genome editing. Plant Cell 28, 1510â1520 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2401">38</a></sup>. Thus, W22 is well positioned to serve as the maize reference inbred for functional genomics studies.</p><p>The annotation of TEs present in the W22 and B73 genome will provide new opportunities to study the sources, and effects, of TE polymorphisms among maize lines. While there are many shared transposons in B73 and W22, there are numerous examples of severe copy number and presence/absence variation of certain families. There are also many examples of polymorphic TE insertions within or near genes that could influence regulation of these genes. Locus-specific studies have provided evidence for the importance of polymorphic TE insertions that influence the regulation of nearby genes, resulting in quantitative trait loci affecting important agronomic traits<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Studer, A., Zhao, Q., Ross-Ibarra, J. &amp; Doebley, J. Identification of a functional transposon insertion in the maize domestication gene tb1. Nat. Genet. 43, 1160â1163 (2011)." href="#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2409">39</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yang, Q. et al. CACTA-like transposable element in ZmCCT attenuated photoperiod sensitivity and accelerated the postdomestication spread of maize. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 110, 16969â16974 (2013)." href="#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2409_1">40</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Castelletti, S., Tuberosa, R., Pindo, M., &amp; Salvi, S. A MITE transposon insertion is associated with differential methylation at the maize flowering time QTL Vgt1. G3 4, 805â812 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2412">41</a></sup>. The ability to have a high structural resolution of both genomes will serve as a resource to enable detailed studies of how transposons influence gene expression among diverse maize genotypes. The knowledge of complex haplotype variation can also be used to inform pan-genome-based approaches that incorporate rearrangements and insertion/deletions from large-scale resequencing studies to augment SNP-based GWAS studies.</p><p>The availability of epigenome profiles for DNA methylation and chromatin accessibility provides an opportunity to study chromatin profiles for thousands of <i>Mu</i> and <i>Ds</i> insertion sites. The differences in these profiles suggest that <i>Mu</i> and <i>Ds</i> utilize distinct targeting mechanisms and occupy different niches of the W22 genome. <i>Mu</i> elements tend to insert in small windows of accessible chromatin that are near highly methylated regions. Previous work has noted the propensity for <i>Mu</i> elements to insert near gene promoters and these regions likely contain the chromatin profiles noted for <i>Mu</i> elements<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Dietrich, C. R. et al. Maize Mu transposons are targeted to the 5Ê¹ untranslated region of the gl8 gene and sequences flanking Mu target-site duplications exhibit nonrandom nucleotide composition throughout the genome. Genetics 160, 697â716 (2002)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2441">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Liu, S. et al. Mu transposon insertion sites and meiotic recombination events co-localize with epigenetic marks for open chromatin across the maize genome. PLoS Genet. 5, e1000733 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2444">30</a></sup>. In contrast, <i>Ds</i> frequently inserts into regions with low CG and CHG methylation that are not enriched for accessible chromatin. This is compatible with the concept of preferential insertion of <i>Ds</i> elements within the coding regions of maize genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Vollbrecht, E. et al. Genome-wide distribution of transposed Dissociation elements in maize. Plant Cell 22, 1667â1685 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2454">13</a></sup>. Interestingly, chromatin profiles for random sites selected within gene promoters or coding regions do not match the profiles observed for <i>Mu</i> or <i>Ds</i> sites. This suggests that <i>Mu</i> and <i>Ds</i> have specific targeting beyond simple selection for promoters or coding regions. Furthermore, the chromatin profiles at loci targeted by active <i>Mu</i> or <i>Ds</i> elements are distinct from the chromatin profiles at sites that flank inactive pre-existing insertion sites. This likely reflects the fact that many of these pre-existing element insertion sites are targeted for silencing and the chromatin properties of the elements themselves can spread into the flanking regions. It is possible that many of the pre-existing TIR families might insert into regions with chromatin profiles similar to <i>Mu</i> or <i>Ds</i> if they were active. As additional high-quality maize genome assemblies become available, comparative analysis of the maize pan-genome is likely to provide further insight into how transposons have shaped the maize genome through the course of domestication.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec6" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec6-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec6">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec6-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">W22 genome sequencing and assembly</h3><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec8">DNA sequencing</h4><p>Genomic DNA was isolated from 10-day-old W22 seedling tissues and nuclear DNA was purified and fractionated from organellar DNA on a CsCl gradient<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Zhang, M. et al. Preparation of megabase-sized DNA from a variety of organisms using the nuclei method for advanced genomics research. Nat. Protoc. 7, 467â478 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2501">42</a></sup>. A genomic library of 800-bp DNA fragment sizes was prepared using the TruSeq DNA Sample Preparation Kit version 2 according to the manufacturerâs protocol (Illumina). A second shotgun library was made using the same kit from DNA template fragments size selected of ~450âbp with no PCR amplification (PCR-free). This fragment size was designed to produce a sequencing overlap of the fragments to be sequenced on the HiSeq2500 v2 Rapid mode as PE sequencing 250ânucleotides per end, thus creating an opportunity to produce âstitchedâ reads of approximately 250âbp to 480âbp in length. Multiple MP libraries were prepared with the objective to increase sequence diversity and genome coverage including three size classes (2â4âkb, 5â7âkb and 8â10âkb) using the Illumina Nextera Mate-Pair Sample Preparation Kit (Illumina). The 800-bp shotgun library and the MP libraries were sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq2500 as PE with 160-bp read lengths for each of the fragments using the SBS sequencing kit version 4. Each library was sequenced to a coverage of ~65âGb (~30Ã the maize genome size). The 450-bp PCR-free shotgun library was sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq2500 using Rapid mode with v2 chemistry as PE with 250-bp read lengths. This library was sequenced to a coverage of ~130âGb (~60Ã the maize genome size). In total, ~180Ãâof sequencing data was produced for this project. All sequencing was conducted at the Roy J. Carver Biotechnology Center (Urbana, IL) at the University of Illinois.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec9">Genome assembly</h4><p>The W22 genome was assembled using DenovoMAGIC 2 (NRGene). Several previous studies have described this assembly approach<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Hirsch, C. N. et al. Draft assembly of elite inbred line PH207 provides insights into genomic and transcriptome diversity in maize. Plant Cell 28, 2700â2714 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2513">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Avni, R. et al. Wild emmer genome architecture and diversity elucidate wheat evolution and domestication. Science 357, 93â97 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2516">43</a></sup> and we provide a summary of the implementation of this approach for W22. Read pre-processing included removal of PCR duplicates, Illumina adaptor AGATCGGAAGAGC and Nextera linkers (for MP libraries). For the 2âÃâ250 450-bp PE libraries, overlapping reads were merged with a minimal required overlap of 10âbp to create stitched reads. Error correction of pre-processed reads was performed by scanning to detect reads with putative sequencing errors (containing a sub-sequence that does not reappear several times in other reads) and those reads were filtered out. The first step of de novo assembly consisted of building a de Bruijn graph (<i>k</i>-merâ=â239âbp) of contigs from the âstitchedâ reads. The large <i>k</i>-mer length significantly reduced the complexity of the de Bruijn graphs, which is essential for high-quality assembly of very complex genomes. No bubble merge and no repeat masking filtering were used.</p><p>To scaffold the contigs of the de Bruijn graph, non-repetitive contigs within the graph were identified and assembled into scaffolds based on mapping information of the âstitchedâ reads. Scaffolding was completed using a directed graph containing scaffolds longer than 200âbp as nodes, and edges were based on the PE and MP links as vertices. Erroneous connections were filtered out to generate unconnected sub-graphs that were ordered into scaffolds. PE reads were used to find reliable paths in the graph for additional repeat resolving. This was accomplished through searching the de Bruijn graph for a unique path connecting pairs of reads mapping to two different scaffolds. The scaffolds were then furthered ordered and linked using the MP libraries, estimating gaps between the contigs according to the distance of MP links. Linking scaffolds with MP reads required confirmation of at least three filtered MPs or at least one filtered MP with supporting confirmation from two or more filter-failed MPs where the Nextera adaptor was not found. Scaffolds shorter than 200âbp were masked and links between non-repetitive contigs mapping to the same scaffolds were united, generating a directed scaffold graph. Further ordering of scaffolds was achieved through alignment of the scaffolds to the B73 reference genome (RefGen_v2) and selection of the most probable genomic location. We did not use the B73 reference to create the W22 scaffolds. The scaffolds were then placed into the pseudomolecules to maximize linear synteny between the assembled and B73 genomes. To improve the accuracy of our scaffolds, we used an independent method to identify and correct misassemblies. We sequenced one HiSeq X10 lane (total of 120G of 150âÃâ2âbp raw sequencing data) with one long-range barcoded DNA library (Chromium system by 10xâgenomics). Reads were mapped to the assembled scaffolds and clusters of reads with the same barcode mapped to adjacent contigs in the scaffolds were identified. Overall, we detected 22 million clusters and the estimated length of &gt;2 million of those molecules is above 50âkb (the estimated average length of all molecules is 15âkb with s.d.â=â23âkb). Next we scanned along each scaffold with a 20-kb-length window and counted: the number of distinct clusters that cover the left edge of the window; the number of distinct clusters that cover the right edge of the window; and the number of distinct clusters that cover the entire window indicating a support for this 20âkb connection by several long molecules (identified by having distinct barcodes). On average, 255 long molecules (s.d.â=â74) cover each edge of the window and 124 long molecules support an entire window (s.d.â=â31). There are 34 potential chimeric scaffolds that were detected as windows with a statistically significant (using a hyper-geometric statistical test with a Bonferroni correction, <i>P</i>âvalue 2.5âÃâ10<sup>â7</sup>) lower number of supporting overlapping long molecules. Twenty-three of these potential chimeric scaffolds were also detected in the genetic marker-based scaffold validation stage (see below). These chimeric scaffolds were broken and reassembled in W22v2 using the X10 information.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec10">Bionano mapping and assembly validation</h4><p>High-molecular-weight genomic DNA was isolated from 0.5âg of W22 leaf tissue using the IrysPrep Plant Tissue DNA Isolation Kit (RE-014-05). The DNA was labeled at Nt.BspQI sites using the IrysPrep NRLS labeling kit (RE-012-10). Molecules were collected using BioNano IrysChips at 100Ã coverage with an average molecule length of 220âkb. The data were then de novo assembled using âoptArgument_humanâ. The resulting W22 assembly contained 1,872 BioNano genome maps (equivalent to contigs) that span a total of 2,171âMb with an N50 of 1.646âMb. After assembly, the W22v2 genome maps were aligned to an in silico BspQI-digested cmap of the NRGene sequence assembly. The final alignment parameter âTotal Unique Aligned Len/Ref Lenâ was 0.983, indicating that 98.3% of the BioNano maps aligned uniquely with the NRGene assembly. As a means to directly compare the quality of the W22 assembly with the quality of the B73 assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Improved maize reference genome with single-molecule technologies. Nature 546, 524â527 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2542">18</a></sup>, we created a hybrid scaffold that incorporated both the sequence and Bionano data. The W22 hybrid assembly contained 97.4% of the Bionano contigs and 99.4% of the sequence assembly.
