These values are slightly better than equivalent values for B73 (95.1% and 98.4%, respectively), indicating that the ordering and orientation of contigs in the NRGene assembly are on par with the PacBio-based B73 reference assembly.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec11">Scaffold validation</h4><p>The 4.4 million high-resolution genetic anchors, which were developed from &gt;14,000 maize inbred lines<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Lu, F. et al. High-resolution genetic mapping of maize pan-genome sequence anchors. Nat. Commun. 6, 6914 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2554">22</a></sup>, were used to validate the scaffolds of the W22 assembly. The sequence anchors were aligned to an initial assembly of W22 using Bowtie2. The scaffolds with at least 20 mapped anchors were examined for the consistency of sequence assembly and genetic position. A total of 409 scaffolds with an overall length of 2.17âGb were evaluated. We found that 23 scaffolds were assembly artifacts, which were then corrected in the W22v2 assembly.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">W22 genome annotation</h3><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec13">W22 gene annotation</h4><p>Annotation of protein-coding genes was performed using MAKER-P pipeline software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Campbell, M. S. et al. MAKER-P: a tool kit for the rapid creation, management, and quality control of plant genome annotations. Plant Physiol. 164, 513â524 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2571">44</a></sup>, with parameters and evidence similar to those recently used to annotate B73<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Improved maize reference genome with single-molecule technologies. Nature 546, 524â527 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2575">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Law, M. et al. Automated update, revision, and quality control of the maize genome annotations using MAKER-P improves the B73 RefGen_v3 gene models and identifies new genes. Plant Physiol. 167, 25â39 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2578">45</a></sup>. Repeat masking (âRepeatMasker Home Pageâ 2017) was performed using exemplar transposon sequences<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Schnable, P. S. et al. The B73 maize genome: complexity, diversity, and dynamics. Science 326, 1112â1115 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2582">28</a></sup> available online at the maize TE database (âMaize Databaseâ 2017). We excluded helitron and MULE elements to avoid false-positive masking from captured exon sequences in such elements. Gene expression evidence included PacBio Iso-seq long reads sequenced from cDNA libraries of six tissues in B73 (<i>n</i>â=â111,151)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Wang, B. et al. Unveiling the complexity of the maize transcriptome by single-molecule long-read sequencing. Nat. Commun. 7, 11708 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2589">46</a></sup>. In addition, we included the following transcriptome assemblies, each processed to exclude short transcripts (&lt;300âbp) and redundancies based on application of CD-HIT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Fu, L., Niu, B., Zhu, Z., Wu, S. &amp; Li, W. CD-HIT: accelerated for clustering the next-generation sequencing data. Bioinformatics 28, 3150â3152 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2594">47</a></sup>: a pooled set of 94 transcriptome assemblies constructed from publicly available RNA-seq reads (<i>n</i>â=â508,233)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Law, M. et al. Automated update, revision, and quality control of the maize genome annotations using MAKER-P improves the B73 RefGen_v3 gene models and identifies new genes. Plant Physiol. 167, 25â39 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2601">45</a></sup>; a transcriptome assembly of B73 seedlings (<i>n</i>â=â112,963)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Martin, J. A. et al. A near complete snapshot of the Zea mays seedling transcriptome revealed from ultra-deep sequencing. Sci. Rep. 4, 4519 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2608">48</a></sup>; a transcriptome assembly of W22 tissues (<i>n</i>â=â589,743). Cross-species evidence was supplied in the form of the following annotated protein files downloaded from Gramene release 46(Gramene FTP)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Tello-Ruiz, M. K. et al. Gramene 2016: comparative plant genomics and pathway resources. Nucleic Acids Res. 44, D1133â40 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2616">49</a></sup>: Arabidopsis_thaliana.TAIR10.27.pep.all.fa;Brachypodium_distachyon.v1.0.27.pep.all.fa; Oryza_sativa.IRGSP-1.0.27.pep.all.fa; Setaria_italica.JGIv2.0.27.pep.all.fa; and Sorghum_bicolor.Sorbi1.27.pep.all.fa. Alignment and downstream processing of sequence evidence to the repeat-masked W22 reference was performed within the MAKER-P pipeline using default parameters. For gene model prediction, the pipeline incorporated AUGUSTUS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Stanke, M. et al. AUGUSTUS: ab initio prediction of alternative transcripts. Nucleic Acids Res. 34, W435âW439 (2006)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2620">50</a></sup> applied with the âmaize5â model and FGENESH<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Salamov, A. A. &amp; Solovyev, V. V. Ab initio gene finding in Drosophila genomic DNA. Genome Res. 10, 516â522 (2000)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2624">51</a></sup> applied with the âmonocotâ model. Stable gene identifiers were assigned using the format Zm00004bXXXXXX (where the Xs represent a random 6-digit number), as specified under âA Standard For Maize Genetics Nomenclatureâ available at MaizeGDB (âMaize Genetics Nomenclatureâ 2017). For further details on the characterization and screening of gene models, see the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec14">TE annotation</h4><p>To identify structurally intact copies of TEs in the genome assembly, we followed a similar approach to that used for B73v4<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Improved maize reference genome with single-molecule technologies. Nature 546, 524â527 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2639">18</a></sup> to characterize features such as target site duplications and terminal motifs. To encourage consistency of nomenclature, we applied the 80-80-80 bioinformatic cutoffs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Wicker, T. et al. A unified classification system for eukaryotic transposable elements. Nat. Rev. Genet. 8, 973â982 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2643">52</a></sup>, to match W22 TE copies to existing families in B73 based on 80% identity across 80% of the length of the TE or its functional sequence, of at least 80 base pairs. For TEs that could not be assigned to a family present in B73, we generated new family names. Each TE is given a unique identifier in the form RLG00001Zm00004b00001, using the Zm00004b identifier for W22 assigned by the maize community nomenclature committee for gene names. This reflects the TE superfamily (RLG, retrotransposon, LTR, Gypsy), a five digit family number (00001), the W22 identifier (Zm00004b) and a unique identifier for this TE copy (00001). For LTR elements, we apply an iterative search of LTRharvest<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Ellinghaus, D., Kurtz, S. &amp; Willhoeft, U. LTRharvest, an efficient and flexible software for de novo detection of LTR retrotransposons. BMC Bioinformatics 9, 18 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2647">53</a></sup>, removing previously identified copies from the genome, and rerunning LTRharvest with the reduced genome. For TIR elements, we utilize TARGeT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Han, Y., Burnette, J. M. 3rd &amp; Wessler, S. R. TARGeT: a web-based pipeline for retrieving and characterizing gene and transposable element families from genomic sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 37, e78 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2651">54</a></sup>, using TE models from the maize TE consortium (MTEC), as well as novel structural families identified using detectMITE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Ye, C., Ji, G. &amp; Liang, C. detectMITE: A novel approach to detect miniature inverted repeat transposable elements in genomes. Sci. Rep. 6, 19688 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2655">55</a></sup>. For helitrons, we use the terminal 30âbp at the 3Ê¹ end to assign to B73 families, and generate new families as needed. For SINE elements, we use SINEfinder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Wenke, T. et al. Targeted identification of short interspersed nuclear element families shows their widespread existence and extreme heterogeneity in plant genomes. Plant Cell 23, 3117â3128 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2660">56</a></sup>, and for LINE elements, we use TARGeT on MTEC TE models. The TE detection pipeline and associated files can be found at github.com/mcstitzer/w22_te_annotation.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec15">Characterization of variation in gene content and arrangement</h4><p>To compare syntenic ortholog retention between W22 and B73, we queried the unmasked W22 genome against the unmasked B73 version 4 from Ensembl release 36 and the outgroup <i>Sorghum bicolor</i> v3.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="McCormick, R. F. et al. The Sorghum bicolor reference genome: improved assembly, gene annotations, a transcriptome atlas, and signatures of genome organization. Plant J. 93, 338â354 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2675">26</a></sup> using CoGeâs SynFind (lastz, 50-gene window, minimum of 5 syntelogs per window), which compares synteny across multiple genomes at once, and then reciprocally compared B73 against W22 and <i>Sorghum</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Determining locally duplicated genes</h3><p>Locally duplicated genes were determined using a method similar to that used to determine the number of shared genes between W22 and B73. The CDS sequences from W22 AGPv2 and B73 AGPv4 genomes were each searched against themselves using COGEâs SynFind tool<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Tang, H. et al. SynFind: compiling syntenic regions across any set of genomes on demand. Genome Biol. Evol. 7, 3286â3298 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2691">57</a></sup>, and the resulting unfiltered LAST search results were downloaded. In each search, LAST hits were cleaned by: removing hits with an e-value of 0.001 or more; condensing hits from multiple transcripts between gene models, to a single hit between gene models (that is, a hit between two gene models was retained if at least one transcript model from each gene model had a valid hit to each other); and removing hits between gene models that are not on the same chromosome. The remaining non-self LAST hits were treated as mappings between paralogs within each inbred genome and were used to determine locally duplicated genes. Distances between genes in the remaining LAST hits were determined by calculating their order and orientation on the chromosome and determining the number of intervening genes. Any gene pair that had less than a maximum of 20 intervening genes was classified as locally duplicated<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Schnable, J. C. Genome evolution in maize: from genomes back to genes. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 66, 329â343 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2695">58</a></sup>.</p><p>Shared and unique tandem duplicates in B73 and W22 were determined by using the master list of ortholog mappings using SynFind. Reciprocal SynFind searches were used where W22 was searched against B73 and vice versa. The reciprocal B73 to W22 gene model mappings that were found in both searches were filtered as syntenic orthologs between the two genomes. Tandem duplications were a subset of the locally duplicated genes that had no intervening genes between them.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Analysis of gene expression</h3><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec18">RNA-seq alignments</h4><p>RNA-seq data for W22 leaf (SRA: <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRR1986376">SRR1986376</a>) were aligned to both the W22 and B73v4 reference genomes. Reads were trimmed using Cutadapt version 1.8.1 and mapped using tophat2 version 2.0.13 to both genomes. Stranded reads were assigned to gene models for each reference using HTSeq version 0.5.3. The counts per gene were normalized to reads per million mapped reads. To compare gene expression values for mappings to the two references, homologus genes were determined from predicted protein sequences for the first isoform of each gene using OrthoFinder version 1.1.8. Only genes with a 1-to-1 match between W22 and B73v4 were retained for comparison.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Alternative splicing analysis</h3><p>W22 RNA-seq reads were trimmed using Cutadapt 1.7.1 to remove adapters and low quality sequences trimmed using Trimmomatic 0.32. Processed reads were then aligned to the genome using STAR 2.5.2 and two pass alignment steps. Sixty-seven RNA-seq sample were aligned to STAR individuals, and splice junction from 67 samples were combined together and only splice junctionâ&gt;â=â3 reads in at least one sample kept. These remaining splice junctions were fed into STAR as guidance and the second alignment was run. Duplicate reads were detected and removed using Picard 2.10.3 MarkDuplicates. Two approaches, Stringtie 1.3.3 and Cufflinks 2.2.1, were used for the genome guided assembly; in addition, we also used de novo transcripts from Trinity. The transcripts built from Cufflinks and Stringtie were filtered using the same approach previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Mei, W. et al. A comprehensive analysis of alternative splicing in paleopolyploid maize. Front. Plant Sci. 8, 694 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2731">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Mei, W., Boatwright, L., Feng, G., Schnable, J. C. &amp; Barbazuk, W. B. Evolutionarily conserved alternative splicing across monocots. Genetics 207, 465â480 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2734">59</a></sup>. To compare alternative splicing in W22 and B73, we took the transcripts from the B73v4 annotation that includes alternative splicing isoforms, and mapped back to the W22v2 genomes and called the alternative splicing loci in B73 relative to the W22v2 genome.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Mapping of <i>Mu</i> and <i>Ds</i> insertion sites</h3><p>Germinal <i>Mu</i> insertions from 13,444 UniformMu lines<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="McCarty, D. R. et al. Steady-state transposon mutagenesis in inbred maize. Plant J. 44, 52â61 (2005)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2756">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Liu, P., McCarty, D. R. &amp; Koch, K. E. Transposon mutagenesis and analysis of mutants in UniformMu maize (Zea mays). Curr. Protoc. Plant Biol. 1, 451â465." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2759">9</a></sup> were mapped in the B73v4 and W22v2 genomes using the Illumina-based MuSeq protocol as described in an earlier study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="McCarty, D. R. et al. Mu-seq: sequence-based mapping and identification of transposon induced mutations. PLoS One 8, e77172 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2763">8</a></sup>. Insertion sites that were assigned unique locations in the W22 genome were analyzed to determine the presence and locations of corresponding sites in the B73v4 genome. Correspondence of insertion sites in W22 and B73 was based on shared MuSeq reads. Insertion sites in W22 identified by MuSeq reads that failed to align to B73 were scored as W22-specific. Flanking <i>Ds</i> (fDs) sequences, which derive from sequenced clones, were placed on each genome assembly using the processing pipeline and placement quality criteria described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Vollbrecht, E. et al. Genome-wide distribution of transposed Dissociation elements in maize. Plant Cell 22, 1667â1685 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2770">13</a></sup>, with minor modifications. For each genome, the pipeline was first applied to the unmasked genome and unmasked fDs sequences, and then the remaining, unplaced fDs sequences were repeat masked and run through the pipeline again with the unmasked genome. Multiple fDs from the same insertion event were collapsed into a single placement when at least one fDs clone included a sequence directly adjacent to the <i>Ds</i> insertion site. Repeat masking used Repeatmasker as described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Vollbrecht, E. et al. Genome-wide distribution of transposed Dissociation elements in maize. Plant Cell 22, 1667â1685 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d50179e2778">13</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">DNA methylation and chromatin accessibility</h3><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec22">Whole-genome bisulfite sequencing and analysis</h4><p>Genomic DNA (1âÂµg) was sheared to a size of 200â300âbp. These DNA fragments were then used to construct a whole-genome bisulfite sequencing library using the KAPA library preparation kit (KK8232). Briefly, the DNA fragments were subjected to end repair, A-tailing, adapter ligation and dual-SPRI size selection following the manufacturerâs instructions. The resulting library, which has a size between 250âbp and 450âbp, was treated with bisulfite sodium so that unmethylated cytosine could be converted to uracil using the Zymo EZ DNA methylation lightning kit (D5031). The KAPA HiFi HotStart Uracilâ+â(KK2801) was used in the PCR reaction with the following program: 95âÂ°C for 2âmin, 8 cycles of 98âÂ°C for 30âs, 60âÂ°C for 30âs, 72âÂ°C for 4âmin and a final extension step at 72Â°C for 10âmin. Finally, the PCR-enriched library was cleaned up using SPRI beads. The library was sequenced using Illumina HiSeq2000 with the PE mode and 100 cycles and the reads are available at <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRX3136383">SRX3136383</a>. Trim_glore was used to trim adapter sequences and read quality was assessed with the default parameters and PE reads mode. Reads that passed quality control were aligned to the W22 assembly using Bismark, allowing at most 1 mismatch in the 25âbp seed sequence (-N 1 -L 25). Duplicate reads were detected and removed using Picard tools. Reads that are uniquely mapped and that are properly paired were used to extract methylation status at each individual cytosine using bismark_methylation_extractor. For PCR inserts that have a size of less than 200âbp (so that the two sequencing reads might overlap), only the first read was used to calculate the methylation level. The file with methylation information for individual cytosines was used to create 100-bp non-overlapping sliding windows across the W22 chromosomes for each of the three sequence contexts, CG, CHG and CHH (Hâ=âA, C or T). Within each 100-bp window, the total number of methylated and unmethylated reads for every cytosine was summed for each sequence context, and methylation levels were derived using the following formula: (no. methylated_reads/(no. methylated_readsâ+âno. unmethylated_reads)).</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec23">MNase assays and analysis</h4><p>Seeds were sown into Fafard 3 soil mix in two 11â Ã 21â Ã 2.5â plastic trays. Nine days after planting, above-ground tissue was cut at the soil line and flash frozen in two batches (one per tray) and stored at â80âÂ°C until grinding. The two batches of frozen seedlings were ground under liquid nitrogen with a mortar and pestle and crosslinked by stirring for 10âmin in 100âml ice-cold fixation buffer (15âmM Pipes-NaOH at pHâ6.8, 0.32âM sorbitol, 80âmM KCl, 20âmM NaCl, 0.5âmM EGTA, 2âmM EDTA, 1âmM dithiothreitol, 0.15âmM spermine and 0.5âmM spermidine) containing 1% formaldehyde. Fixation was stopped by adding glycine to 125âmM. Nuclei were isolated by adding Triton X-100 to 1% final by the addition of 0.1âvol of a 10% (vol/vol) Triton X-100 stock, followed by stirring for 10âmin. The suspension was filtered through one layer of Miracloth (Calbiochem) and placed in 50âml centrifuge tubes. In these centrifuge tubes, 35âml nuclear suspensions were underlaid with a 15âml Percoll cushion composed of 50% (vol/vol) Percoll (GE) in BFA. Nuclei suspensions were centrifuged at 3,000<i>g</i> for 15âmin at 4âÂ°C. The nuclei at the Percoll interface were transferred to a 50âml tube and diluted twofold with MNase digestion buffer (50âmM Tris-HCl at pHâ7.5, 320âmM sucrose, 4âmM MgCl<sub>2</sub> and 1âmM CaCl<sub>2</sub>). Nuclei suspensions were centrifuged at 2,000<i>g</i> for 10âmin at 4âÂ°C, and nuclei pellets were resuspended in 2.5âml MNase digestion buffer. Nuclei were aliquoted into 500âml aliquots, flash-frozen in liquid nitrogen and stored at â80âÂ°C. Nuclei were thawed at room temperature and digested by adding MNase to 10âUâml<sup>â1</sup> (light) or 100âUâml<sup>â1</sup> (heavy), and incubated at room temperature for 5âmin. Digestions were stopped with 10âmM EGTA. Nuclei were de-crosslinked by incubation overnight at 65âÂ°C in the presence of 1% SDS and 100âÎ¼gâml<sup>â1</sup> proteinase K. DNA was extracted by phenolâchloroform extraction followed by ethanol precipitation. Digested DNA was resuspended in 40âÎ¼gâml<sup>â1</sup> RNAse A and size-selected with a two-sided Ampure XP bead purification step (0.5Ã beads:DNA followed by 2.5Ã beads:DNA). Size-selected DNA was used to prepare sequencing libraries using the NEBNext Ultra DNA Library Prep Kit for Illumina (NEB), using the manufacturer's instructions. Indexed libraries were pooled and sequenced on four Illumina HiSeq 2500 lanes with PE 50-cycle sequencing. Data are available in NCBI SRA (accession <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRP118121">SRP118121</a>).</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec24">Metaplots for chromatin surrounding genes or TE insertion sites</h4><p>For DNA methylation, the context-specific levels of DNA methylation for each 100-bp bin across the genome were utilized. For chromatin accessibility, the normalized (reads per million) values for the heavy and light digest were calculated for each 100-bp bin and the ratio (heavy/light) was used as a measure of relative accessibility. For each annotated attribute (gene, TIR, LTR), bedtools closest was used to calculate the distance of each 100-bp bin to that attribute. Relative distance was then determined for the 100-bp bins within the attribute (normalized to a 1,000-bp window). All 100-bp bin data for 1âkb upstream and downstream of the attribute were retained and data points were averaged for every 100-bp window; these values were then plotted across the genomic region relative to the gene, TIR or LTR attribute. Metaplots of chromatin features surrounding potential <i>Ds</i> or <i>Mu</i> insertion sites were performed by selecting the 100-bp bins upstream and downstream (10âkb) of each insertion site using bedtools. Data points were averaged for each 100-bp window across the genomic space relative to the insertion site (indicated by <i>x</i>-axis zero).</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec25">Analysis of chromatin at edges of TIR TE families</h4><p>The DNA methylation and MNase data for 100-bp bins were compared with the TIR annotation gff using bedtools closest to identify the 100-bp bins within 4âkb of each TIR element. Only 100-bp bins that are closest to a single TIR element are used for analysis of chromatin properties at the edge of TIR elements. The TE family ID was identified and for each family (only those families with 10+âmembers were used to generate the figure; see Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0#MOESM1">8</a> for details on these families), the average level of DNA methylation or chromatin accessibility was calculated for each 100-bp window with respect to the TE edge. <i>Mu</i> and <i>Ds</i> data were then added for each DNA methylation context and MNase. The heatmap was then generated using a Euclidean clustering system focusing on the CG and CHG methylation levels. Once the family order was determined, a separate scale was used to generate each context of methylation and MNase to view the trend within each (CG/CHGâ=â0â1, CHHâ=â0â0.15, MNaseâ=â0â4).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec26">Reporting Summary</h3><p>Further information on experimental design is available in the Nature Research Reporting Summary linked to this article.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec27">Data availability</h3><p>The W22v2 genome sequence is available at NCBI under the accession <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/assembly/GCA_001644905.2/">GCA_001644905.2</a>. The W22 annotations are available at <a href="http://MaizeGDB.org">MaizeGDB.org</a>. There are also RNA-seq (<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRR1986376">SRR1986376</a>), whole-genome bisulfite (<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRX3136383">SRX3136383</a>) and MNase (<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRP118121">SRP118121</a>) data sets available at NCBI SRA.</p></div></div></section>

                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="FPar1">Competing interests</h3>
                <p>Several authors are employed by companies that provide genome sequencing and assembly support. O.B., K.B., G.B.-Z., P.C., G.K., G.R., D.S.-T. and I.S. are employed by NRGene and J.S. is employed by BioNano Genomics.</p>
              
            </div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="additional-information" data-title="Additional information"><div class="c-article-section" id="additional-information-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="additional-information">Additional information</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="additional-information-content"><p><b>Publisherâs note:</b> Springer Nature remains neutral with regard to jurisdictional claims in published maps and institutional affiliations.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec29" data-title="Integrated supplementary information"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec29-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec29">Integrated supplementary information</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec29-content"><div data-test="supplementary-info"><div id="figshareContainer" class="c-article-figshare-container" data-test="figshare-container"></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig6"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0/figures/6" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-018-0158-0/MediaObjects/41588_2018_158_Fig6_ESM.jpg">Supplementary Figure 1 Comparison of the completeness of the B73v4 and W22v2 genome annotations.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>The completeness of the genome assemblies and genome annotations was assessed by benchmarking a universal single-copy orthologous gene set (BUSCO) (SimÃ£o et al. 2015). The relative frequencies of complete single-copy genes, duplicated genes, fragmented genes and missing genes were very similar in the B73v4 and W22v2 genomes.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig7"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0/figures/7" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-018-0158-0/MediaObjects/41588_2018_158_Fig7_ESM.jpg">Supplementary Figure 2 Density plot of shared and unique alternative splicing of W22 compared to B73v4.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>For the B73v4 alternative splicing events, we mapped the isoforms from B73v4 annotation to the W22 genome and identified the alternative splicing events. We classified the common alternative splicing events based on the coordinates of the alternative region relative to the W22v2 genome.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig8"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0/figures/8" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-018-0158-0/MediaObjects/41588_2018_158_Fig8_ESM.jpg">Supplementary Figure 3 Improved transcriptome analyses using the W22v2 genome.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>a</b>, RNA-seq data derived from endosperm tissue of W22 (SRA: <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRR1986376">SRR1986376</a>) were aligned to both B73v4 and W22v2 using the same parameters. The percentage of mapped reads improves by mapping the data to W22. <b>b</b>, A set of 20,994 syntenic orthologous genes in B73 and W22 were identified and used for comparison of expression levels in alignments to B73 or W22. A comparison of expression level (reads per million, RPM) shows generally similar estimates in both genotypes with some genes that have differing expression estimates depending upon which genome was used for a reference. <b>c</b>â<b>f</b>, Differences in RPKM estimates can result from differences in annotation or mapping efficiency. <b>c</b>,<b>d</b>, Overlapping gene models in B73 result in all reads mapping to Zm00001d028756 (orange transcript) to be called ambiguous, while the corresponding gene in W22, Zm00004b001227 (orange transcript), has reads assigned. The adjacent gene, Zm00004b001228 in W22 and Zm00001d028757 in B73 (teal transcripts), has the same number of reads assigned to each reference, with a lower RPKM value reported in B73 owing to the longer gene model. <b>e</b>,<b>f</b>, The RPKM value for genes Zm00004b005439 in W22 and Zm00001d034453 in B73 is higher when mapping to W22 (<b>e</b>) than to B73 (<b>f</b>) owing to improved alignment to several regions of the gene (marked in yellow). Mapped reads are colored by strand: blue, forward; red, reverse.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig9"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0/figures/9" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-018-0158-0/MediaObjects/41588_2018_158_Fig9_ESM.jpg">Supplementary Figure 4 Comparison of TIR copy number in B73 and W22.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>a</b>, The proportion of TIR families in each of the superfamilies (Activator (DTA); CACTA (DTC), PIF/Harbinger (DTH), Mutator (DTM), Tourist (DTT)) was determined for all TIR families (blue) in the B73 and W22 genomes. The proportion of TIR families in these categories was then determined for B73-specific (orange) and W22-specific (gray) families. <b>b</b>, The copy number in each TIR TE family is shown for B73 and W22. Color indicates superfamilies. <b>c</b>, The relative copy number for each LTR TE family in B73 and W22 is shown. In <b>d</b>, only families with &lt;500 copies are shown. <b>e</b>, Boxplot of the percent identity of LTR sequences for LTR retrotransposons, demonstrating that elements in B73-specific families with at least five members (orange) are younger than members of shared families (blue) and elements in B73-specific families with fewer than five members (red). Line, median; box limits, first and third quartiles; whiskers, furthest point within 1.5 * IQR; points, outliers.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig10"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0/figures/10" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-018-0158-0/MediaObjects/41588_2018_158_Fig10_ESM.jpg">Supplementary Figure 5 Profiles of CHH methylation surrounding sites targeted by <i>Ds</i> (solid lines) or <i>Mu</i> (dashed lines).</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>The level of CHH methylation is shown for the flanking regions (up to 10 kb) near <i>Ds</i> or <i>Mu</i> insertion sites.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig11"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0/figures/11" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-018-0158-0/MediaObjects/41588_2018_158_Fig11_ESM.jpg">Supplementary Figure 6 Dendrogram analysis (unrooted tree) of terpene synthases in B73 and W22.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>A set of 81 amino acid sequences (42 from B73 in blue and 39 from W22 in black) were used to generate a tree based on MUSCLE protein alignment by using the Maximum Likelihood method and a previously described substitution model. Bootstrap values (<i>n</i> = 1,000 replicates) are shown next to each node. The tree is drawn to scale, with branch lengths measured in the number of substitutions per site. All positions with less than 80% site coverage were eliminated. Evolutionary analyses were conducted in MEGA6. Asterisks are used to mark likely non-functional genes.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig12"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0/figures/12" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-018-0158-0/MediaObjects/41588_2018_158_Fig12_ESM.jpg">Supplementary Figure 7 Sequence conservation among Mutator transposable elements.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>a</b>, Diagram of a Mutator transposable element. The highly conserved terminal inverted repeats (TIRs) can be used to identify Mu elements and to categorize them based on phylogenetic groups. <b>b</b>, Alignment of consensus sequences for each of the seven Mu TIR groups. Highlighted nucleotides indicate disagreements with the overall consensus sequence. The terminal 20 positions are the most conserved across TIR groups. The predicted transposase-binding site (from position 34 to 68) is also highly conserved (57%) across the seven groups.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig13"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41588-018-0158-0/figures/13" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-018-0158-0/MediaObjects/41588_2018_158_Fig13_ESM.jpg">Supplementary Figure 8 Analysis of synteny between <i>Mutator</i> transposons identified in W22 and B73, including intact elements and orphan TIRs.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Of the 386 Mu elements or orphan TIRs examined, 133 were present in both the W22 and B73 genomes.</p></div></div></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec30" data-title="Supplementary information"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec30-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec30">Supplementary information</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec30-content"><div data-test="supplementary-info">
